<div id="review_1348366556" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1348366556" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1348366556?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/10431656-lucifer-s-hammer">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who like hard sci-fi</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      Came with a Larry Niven collection
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1348366556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8120404599748216930"><b>A disaster and post-apocalyptic novel rolled into one</b><br/>10 August 2015<br/><br/>	Well I am finally back from my trip after finally finishing this monster of a book 20 minutes before my plane touched down at Melbourne Airport (and what a horrid flight it was: I really should have put my carry on bag into the overhead locker - I so didn't need my laptop because I was too exhausted to do anything other than attempt to finish this book, despite the fact that the plane had to take the long way round due to a volc</span>
  <span id="freeText8120404599748216930" style="display:none"><b>A disaster and post-apocalyptic novel rolled into one</b><br>10 August 2015<br><br>	Well I am finally back from my trip after finally finishing this monster of a book 20 minutes before my plane touched down at Melbourne Airport (and what a horrid flight it was: I really should have put my carry on bag into the overhead locker - I so didn't need my laptop because I was too exhausted to do anything other than attempt to finish this book, despite the fact that the plane had to take the long way round due to a volcano in Bali). Anyway, I have to say that I am surprised that it took me so long to read this book, especially since I was on holidays because I tend to get through books much quicker than that (though this book does drag quite a bit in places – they really could have cut a chunk of it out – it really didn't need to be a soap opera). However there are two things I discovered:<br><br>1) Don't think you can read a book on a speed boat in Thailand during the wet season: not only will you be bouncing all over the place, when you hit that first rainstorm you get absolutely drenched and you can pretty much say bye bye book (I didn't because I thought it would be a little anti-social, which for some reason playing on your mobile phone wasn't, though I was surprised that I could actually get internet in some of the places I visited).<br><br>2) Don't expect to be able to read a book in a bar in Patong Beach: okay, they do have some normal bars, but pretty much you will find it impossible to do so in all of the others. When I first arrived I went to the nearest bar, ordered a beer, and went to sit at a table to read the book. However the two bar girls were surprised that I didn't want to sit at the bar, so when I sat at the table two of them rushed out and sat down next to me: oh, you reading book? what book you reading? I can't read!<br><br>In fact there was one bar that I went into and the <a href="http://www.urbandictionary.com/define.php?term=Mamasang&amp;defid=5502383" rel="nofollow noopener">mamasang</a> simply refused to let me read. She kept on waving a tissue between me and the book and doing her best to distract me (which worked quite successfully). Okay, I probably could have stayed in my hotel but seriously, with all of the music blasting in through my window at night it is really hard not to say “what the hell am I doing in here? I should be out there”. Let's just say in the end I gave up knowing that none of the girls in any of the bars were going to let me have some peace and quiet (unless, of course, I went to the normal bars, but seriously they are nowhere near as fun).<br><br>	Anyway, enough of that because it is not my intention to talk about Patong Beach or my holiday – I have another blog where I will be doing that (and since I have only just got home, and have quite a bit more to write about it, I won't be giving you a link – yet).<br><br>	At first I thought this book was awesome. In fact as I was reading it I thought the book was so good that I was surprised that they haven't made a movie starring Dwanye Johnson:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439202665i/15807498.jpg" width="174" height="258" alt="San Andreas Poster" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>or Bruce Willis:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439202665i/15807499.jpg" width="182" height="256" alt="Armageddon Movie" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	Okay, in many cases it would probably be just another disaster movie, however most disaster movies call it quits straight after the disaster and the survivors (who you can work out pretty quickly because they tend to be the stars) say 'gee, we managed to survive, everything is going to be okay now'. Lucifer's Hammer goes much further than this as the authors explore not just the immediate aftermath but also the impact the disaster would have on the modern world. Mind you, it took me a little while to work out how it was going to end, especially during the time when they were attempting to pick up the pieces, but it became ever clear with the discovery of a still active nuclear power plant and the rise of a fanatical group of cannibals. Lucifer's Hammer is not just a book about a comet hitting the Earth, it is a disaster book and a post-apocalyptic adventure rolled into one.<br><br>	I thought the use of a comet instead of an asteroid was much better, but my suspicion was that an asteroid would pretty much destroy the world (and they suggested that all of the asteroids that could hit the Earth have already done so). Also comets are notoriously hard to destroy (not that they could have easily done it in 1977, though they still could have sent a lunar lander out there and planted some nuclear devices similar to what they did in Armageddon). However the thing about comets is that out in the ort cloud they are simply lumps of ice, but as they approach the sun they transform into the object that we are all familiar with (and I also find it fascinating that the comet's tail always points away from the sun).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439202665i/15807500._SX540_.jpg" width="340" height="258" alt="Comet" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	The thing with comets is that they are made up of millions of chunks of ice so even if the comet doesn't hit the Earth but the Earth still passes through the coma (the area around the comet) it still has the ability to have a huge impact (and as the authors suggested create an extinction level event, which also suggested that the Brown-Hamner comet was what wiped out the dinosaurs). Their portrait of what would actually happen when the comet passes by the Earth was also interesting since it didn't just cause huge tidal waves but also resulted in numerous Earthquakes and causing pretty much all of the volcanoes to erupt. The massive amounts of water that went up into the atmosphere would also result in almost perpetual rain – I sort of wondered whether the story of Noah's Ark is a report of such an event.<br><br>	However the aftermath was also quite interesting. It wasn't just the survivor's struggle to last out the winter as well as trying to save as much of civilisation as possible, but also how such disasters can result in the rise of fanatics. This was described in the form of the New Brotherhood, which began as simply a group of soldiers resorting to cannibalism and then turning into a fanatical religious group which was growing by leaps and bounds. It is also interesting that this group is rabidly anti-technology believing not just that it was humanity's technology that brought about the destruction of the world, but that by allowing this technology to remain then the world will simply go back to the way it was before where people are effectively enslaved.<br><br>	What we are seeing is the conflict being played out between those who wish to rebuild and those who wish to remake the world into something new. It is interesting that the whole 'fight the power' group identifies with the cannibals and the luddites (those who hate technology) though I suspect that this has a lot more to do with when it was written, since we are looking at the period shortly after the civil rights movement and the rise of the black panthers. However the authors seem to connect this more with the black militant groups as opposed to left-wing radical groups such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weather_Underground" rel="nofollow noopener">Weather Underground</a><a></a>.<br><br>	The biggest problem that I found with this book though is that it starts to get a little like a soap opera, especially near the end. In fact even though it does pace well, it felt as if it was a little too long in places. Okay, granted, I had quite a few distractions in the time that I was reading it, however I did feel that it could have been cut down quite a lot, especially since parts of it do seem to drag.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8120404599748216930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3032490254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3032490254" />
    <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud"><img alt="Oleksandr Zholud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506182069p2/4743119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3032490254?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oleksandr Zholud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oleksandr Zholud" href="/user/show/4743119-oleksandr-zholud">Oleksandr Zholud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/10306190-lucifer-s-hammer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3032490254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11252008752995165012">This is a large SF apoc/post-apoc novel. I read is as a part of monthly reading for July 2020 at <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/109465-the-evolution-of-science-fiction' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Evolution of Science Fiction</a> group and in a month (in August 2020) it should be in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hugo & Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group. The book was first published in 1977 and was nominated for Hugo, but lost to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218427.Gateway__Heechee_Saga___1_' title='Gateway (Heechee Saga, #1) by Frederik Pohl' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gateway</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22996.Frederik_Pohl' title='Frederik Pohl' rel='nofollow noopener'>Frederik Pohl</a>.<br/><br/>The story’s title is a name of the comet, which should go right next to Earth in 1977. Originally the comet was named Hamner-Brown Comet by the names of the discoverers, bu</span>
  <span id="freeText11252008752995165012" style="display:none">This is a large SF apoc/post-apoc novel. I read is as a part of monthly reading for July 2020 at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/109465-the-evolution-of-science-fiction" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Evolution of Science Fiction</a> group and in a month (in August 2020) it should be in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/474371-hugo-nebula-awards-best-novels" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugo &amp; Nebula Awards: Best Novels</a> group. The book was first published in 1977 and was nominated for Hugo, but lost to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/218427.Gateway__Heechee_Saga___1_" title="Gateway (Heechee Saga, #1) by Frederik Pohl" rel="nofollow noopener">Gateway</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22996.Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl" rel="nofollow noopener">Frederik Pohl</a>.<br /><br />The story’s title is a name of the comet, which should go right next to Earth in 1977. Originally the comet was named Hamner-Brown Comet by the names of the discoverers, but Hammer sounds so much better! The first roughly third of the novel introduces a horde of characters, quite unusual for the SFF at the times of writing, 41 persons listed as Dramatis Personae as there are few missing! They are different people of different status and profession, from Tim Hamner, a wealthy hobbyist astronomer, who co-discovered the comet to a journalist team that covers the story, to a professional criminal, a sex maniac, a solder, a postman, a congressman, scientists, astronauts and many more. <br /><br />It should be noted that the majority of actors are white men, while women are more in the role of a trophy wife or secretary and majority of black people are with criminals or movements like Black Panthers. Moreover, right after the calamity there is a widespread practice of teenage wives, who prefer to get a husband to likely rape/death. I highly doubt that the new SF book with such depictions (even while there are strong women and positive blacks) would have been published, and for sure it wouldn’t have been nominated for Hugo. It is fascinating how times change!<br /><br />The first third doesn’t sound like a SF, more a contemporary fiction with some mention of space program and comets, but nothing out of place. Moreover, most actors are sure that the comet will miss, up to the very last day and if the novel would have been shorter, I’d believe that it is a story about unsubstantiated human panic.<br /><br />As the comet hits both land and sea, the global catastrophe starts, described in painstakingly small details. The authors definitely made their research and part of it maybe seen in epigraphs before each chapter, taken from such books as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/443305.How_the_World_Will_End" title="How the World Will End by Daniel Cohen" rel="nofollow noopener">How the World Will End</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3486247.The_Coming_Dark_Age" title="The Coming Dark Age by Roberto Vacca" rel="nofollow noopener">The Coming Dark Age</a>. <br /><br />An interesting novel with well written calamity and people rapidly abandoning veneer of civilization, but I’d prefer less characters, shorter, more straight-lined story.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11252008752995165012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_130441482" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130441482" />
    <a title="Bandit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit"><img alt="Bandit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289488158p2/4528663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130441482?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bandit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bandit" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit">Bandit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780449235997" href="/book/show/993409.Lucifer_s_Hammer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130441482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4339388615518031424">In my quest to read classic post apocalyptic classics, I couldn't very well ignore the famous Lucifer's Hammer, although having read it, I now wish I did. Any book opening with a list of characters (and not being Shakespeare) should warn the reader of how difficult it'll be to keep those characters straight, which was definitely the case here. It might have been easier, had the characters been more likeable, but they were just a bit like stereotypes and not very relatable. The introductions took</span>
  <span id="freeText4339388615518031424" style="display:none">In my quest to read classic post apocalyptic classics, I couldn't very well ignore the famous Lucifer's Hammer, although having read it, I now wish I did. Any book opening with a list of characters (and not being Shakespeare) should warn the reader of how difficult it'll be to keep those characters straight, which was definitely the case here. It might have been easier, had the characters been more likeable, but they were just a bit like stereotypes and not very relatable. The introductions took so long, it was hundreds of pages before any action even took place. Once the Hammer falls (and this book was written years before Hammer time, so none of those jokes work) the book becomes more interesting and more entertaining, but it is still so slow and cumbersome to read. I'm all for epics, but a post apocalyptic epic of that size with that many characters should be as good as The Passage to make it worth while. This book was interesting in parts, decently written, with some good developments, a lot of racism and a lot of detailed information about comets and also surprisingly not that dated considering its age (probably the least dated older book I have read), but overall just not enjoyable enough to warrant the time it took to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4339388615518031424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1187318563" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15132076062735928366"><br/>3.5 <br/><br/>What could possibly be worse than a full scale nuclear war? How about a century long ice age covering what is left of civilization after a massive comet strike. <br/><br/>I felt like the characters were a little weak... unrealistic in their mannerisms and dialogue, but the plot line was powerful. Of course the final events would take place around my home town of Bakersfield California. The scariest part about stories like this one is that it is something that could actually happen, and in our present </span>
  <span id="freeText15132076062735928366" style="display:none"><br />3.5 <br /><br />What could possibly be worse than a full scale nuclear war? How about a century long ice age covering what is left of civilization after a massive comet strike. <br /><br />I felt like the characters were a little weak... unrealistic in their mannerisms and dialogue, but the plot line was powerful. Of course the final events would take place around my home town of Bakersfield California. The scariest part about stories like this one is that it is something that could actually happen, and in our present state of technology there is not a damn thing we could do to stop it. <br /><br />This was a long book, over 600 pages, but other than the introduction of all the major players which took place in the first part of the novel, the book was an attention grabber. Of course there were a couple of spots where some romantic interlude had to be tediously described, and if you're like me, you could do without it. For Christs sake, just say "on such and such night, so and so fucked" and get on with the story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15132076062735928366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14001967548271282232">2020-03-02 - I just saw a friend reference this book and realized I had not reviewed it, but really liked it. I read this about 20-24 years ago or so and found it totally gripping. I did not like the negative implications and authoritarian nature of the authors' dealing with the apocalyptic scenario they had drawn, but they still did a highly plausible job. <br/><br/>The set-up: What happens to some people in the LA area (including the one who actually discovered the upcoming disaster) when a meteor/aster</span>
  <span id="freeText14001967548271282232" style="display:none">2020-03-02 - I just saw a friend reference this book and realized I had not reviewed it, but really liked it. I read this about 20-24 years ago or so and found it totally gripping. I did not like the negative implications and authoritarian nature of the authors' dealing with the apocalyptic scenario they had drawn, but they still did a highly plausible job. <br /><br />The set-up: What happens to some people in the LA area (including the one who actually discovered the upcoming disaster) when a meteor/asteroid hits earth? Several gruesome scenes and practical advice scenes. Some savage plot devices and predictions. Heroism and cowardice. Battle scenes. Fights for survival and giving up.<br /><br />Very compelling book. Prediction: you may be forever changed after reading this. And you will look at the risks of meteors and asteroids hitting the earth as WAY more immediate and significant than AGW (Anthropomorphic Global Warming - ie. "climate change").</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23841736-sarah?shelf=audio">audio</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1382775069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6811100787609808246">Towards the beginning of this book I knew I was going to give it five stars and I figured that one star would be for nostalgia. Then I realized that any book that keeps me going back over and over for nearly 25 years has damn well earned the full five stars. I just love this book. It's what made me fall in love with the post-apocalyptic sub-genre in the first place.<br/><br/>35% of the book takes place before the comet strike. One of the thing that always interests me is how very angry people get towards </span>
  <span id="freeText6811100787609808246" style="display:none">Towards the beginning of this book I knew I was going to give it five stars and I figured that one star would be for nostalgia. Then I realized that any book that keeps me going back over and over for nearly 25 years has damn well earned the full five stars. I just love this book. It's what made me fall in love with the post-apocalyptic sub-genre in the first place.<br /><br />35% of the book takes place before the comet strike. One of the thing that always interests me is how very angry people get towards Hamner when they realize a strike is likely. They blame him for inventing the comet! It's absurd but at the same time I can see it happening. I feel like Niven and Pournelle cover just about every possible option for the types of communities and groups that spring up. They do a really great job showing people trying to survive the initial strikes, and all the horrors that go along with that. It does follow a pretty huge cast of characters but they all converge over the course of the book. I think it works well.<br /><br />I listened to the audio this time and it was well done. I thoroughly enjoyed it but I did miss the paperback copy I used to have. I've read it too many times to just toss it aside in favor of the audio. But it was enjoyable and the narrator did a great job. I would recommend the book over the audio but it's mostly for nostalgic reasons.<br /><br />25 hours of audio and I finally got to hear that last line: "You can fly, but we control the lightning!" I love this book :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6811100787609808246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781455883974" href="/book/show/15022860-lucifer-s-hammer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3440960948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15498904695753760432">The end of our civilization comes as a comet hits the world & we're shown through the eyes of a bunch in California. This book has it all & is well researched. I first read it when it came out in the 1970s & am surprised by how well it has aged - very well indeed. Perennial problems with race, religion, & the morals of survival are all examined through a large, but well drawn cast of characters.<br/><br/>I read this with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21563906' rel='nofollow noopener'>Evolution of SF</a> group & it was a great pick since I can see where it helped spawn</span>
  <span id="freeText15498904695753760432" style="display:none">The end of our civilization comes as a comet hits the world &amp; we're shown through the eyes of a bunch in California. This book has it all &amp; is well researched. I first read it when it came out in the 1970s &amp; am surprised by how well it has aged - very well indeed. Perennial problems with race, religion, &amp; the morals of survival are all examined through a large, but well drawn cast of characters.<br /><br />I read this with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21563906" rel="nofollow noopener">Evolution of SF</a> group &amp; it was a great pick since I can see where it helped spawn several excellent other SF novels including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/889284.The_Postman" title="The Postman by David Brin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Postman</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4922079.One_Second_After__After___1_" title="One Second After (After, #1) by William R. Forstchen" rel="nofollow noopener">One Second After</a>. It also examined some hard truths about how much we take for granted. The end was fantastic: "Give my children the lightening." Wow. Yes that's a bit of a spoiler, but you'll need to read the book to find out just how much is held in that small sentence. If you haven't, I highly suggest you do.<br /><br />I don't think this is the correct audio edition. I'm pretty sure mine is from the 1980s or 1990s &amp; was ripped off a cassette tape I got used from the library. Well done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15498904695753760432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241706633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10447203647973571691">Some books take you back to a time and place. I can tell you I was 17. It was spring break. My senior year of high school. I was in New Mexico. At an artists house. A dinner party. I sat in the corner reading this book. <br/><br/>This was a most enjoyable read. I liked the build up, the various vignettes of people. I loved the detailed description of the end of the world. I loved all the little (well, not literally little) things that went down with our characters, how they survived or didn't. I loved the</span>
  <span id="freeText10447203647973571691" style="display:none">Some books take you back to a time and place. I can tell you I was 17. It was spring break. My senior year of high school. I was in New Mexico. At an artists house. A dinner party. I sat in the corner reading this book. <br /><br />This was a most enjoyable read. I liked the build up, the various vignettes of people. I loved the detailed description of the end of the world. I loved all the little (well, not literally little) things that went down with our characters, how they survived or didn't. I loved the end or the beginning, it depends upon how you look at it I suppose. <br /><br />There were some strong female characters. Some may disagree, but I argue that one should look at history and the time the story was written and took place in. Go Eileen, you were a bad ass. <br /><br />Honestly, I became nostalgic for those good ole days. <br /><br />If you like end of the world drama this is it. Get past your current notions and revisit the past. I mean, it is just a story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10447203647973571691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3807064686367017577" style="display:none">A bit dated in language and attitudes, possessed of a few too many convenient coincidences, and stretching credibility more than once (cannibalism so quickly?? surely not!), nevertheless still a classic of the genre and a fun read. <div id="review_279177718" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/279177718" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7240015-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485740487p2/7240015.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/279177718?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/7240015-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=doomsday">doomsday</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7240015-stephen?shelf=the-end-of-the-world-as-we-know-it">the-end-of-the-world-as-we-know-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer279177718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11580942358941718625">The eeriest part of the story of the dinosaurs is its sudden, abrupt, and once-mysterious ending. After nearly 200 million years of domination, the dinosaurs vanished in a startlingly moment. Although the source of this mass extinction was debated hotly for years, today a general consensus of scientists believes asteroid impact to have been the culprit. The force of the impact shockwave would have been disastrous by itself, vaporizing everything in a wide radius...but the widespread ecological d</span>
  <span id="freeText11580942358941718625" style="display:none">The eeriest part of the story of the dinosaurs is its sudden, abrupt, and once-mysterious ending. After nearly 200 million years of domination, the dinosaurs vanished in a startlingly moment. Although the source of this mass extinction was debated hotly for years, today a general consensus of scientists believes asteroid impact to have been the culprit. The force of the impact shockwave would have been disastrous by itself, vaporizing everything in a wide radius...but the widespread ecological disruption and climate change which followed doomed the survivors, especially those who were adapted for certain ecological niches. Imagine, then, the fate of hyperspecialized humans following a similar impact. What becomes of us, a species most of whose members are far removed from the production of food, who are utterly dependent on an evermore fragile castle of cards called civilization, when that structure collapses? Such is the setup of the terrifying disaster thriller Lucifer's Hammer, easily the best of its genre I have ever read...or can imagine.<br /><br />The year is 1977, and the Cold War is about to end...for the participants are doomed. The discovery of a new comet delights the astronomical community and general public, especially seeing as it will pass near enough to the Earth to provide a fantastic light show but not too close as to pose a threat. But no one's data is perfect, and the comet -- dubbed The Hammer of Lucifer -- does fall. Multiple impact points vaporize land and ocean alike, and the force of the hit triggers massive earthquakes and global volcanic activity. Tsunamis and torrential rains follow, and the astronauts orbiting Earth can only watch in horror as chaos engulfs the globe, civilization goes dark, and the Earth itself becomes clouded over -- no longer a 'pale blue dot', the planet is swathed in stormclouds which will deliver a harvest-killing rain of destruction.<br /><br />On the ground, beneath those clouds, there are survivors. Those who live through Hammerfall race toward the high ground like rats fleeing a sinking ship. Once they were civilized, and their shoulders bore a thousand petty burdens -- what to wear, which car to purchase next? -- but now they were reduced to scrounging for food and shelter. Rich and poor, powerful and weak, black and white -- no one is spared from the basic struggle of survival. As the weeks pass and the immediate damages are over, the scattered survivors form groups and learn that their greatest enemy may be one another, for the disaster's fallout has allowed some of the most base and savage instincts of humanity to express themselves in full. Although readers get hints of what is happening around the world, most of the action is confined to the San Joaquin Valley of California. As in Stephen King's The Stand, the survivors coalesce into two groups, and their interests collide in the fate of a nuclear power plant which somehow survived the catastrophe and may represent humanity's best hope for recovery.<br /><br />Virtually every natural disaster known to us is unleashed by the Hammer, but I was less interested in the race for immediate survival during the fall itself than by the aftermath. How do people survive the coming winter, let alone the coming ice age? Lucifer's Hammer abounds in characters, and watching them struggle to regain civilization -- and collapse into depravity -- is utterly gripping. People are forced to assume leadership, to find a place for themselves in this newly devastated world. Death is all around them, and their futures are utterly uncertain. It's an ideal foundation for a novel in which human beings struggle against the elements and the worst of themselves, seeking to overcome it all.<br /><br />This is one SF thriller I highly recommend; this makes the Mayan doomsday hype look pale by comparison.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11580942358941718625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4567226154114697965">One of the classic TEOTWAWKI ("The End of the World As We Know It") novels about a comet striking the Earth, published in 1977 and thus predating films like "Armageddon" and "Deep Impact" by a good solid decade.<br/><br/>A gripping, scientifically well-grounded, frighteningly realistic storyline and a diverse cast of characters that hold the reader's interest.   <span id="freeText4567226154114697965" style="display:none">One of the classic TEOTWAWKI ("The End of the World As We Know It") novels about a comet striking the Earth, published in 1977 and thus predating films like "Armageddon" and "Deep Impact" by a good solid decade.<br /><br />A gripping, scientifically well-grounded, frighteningly realistic storyline and a diverse cast of characters that hold the reader's interest. And as someone who grew up in L.A, the SoCal locations--particularly the San Fernando Valley--make the novel relatable for me at a personal level; it was first recommended to me back in 1985 by two real-life Jet Propulsion Lab (JPL) employees....and JPL factors prominently in the plot.<br /><br />RANDOM STREAM OF CONSCIOUSNESS NOTES AND OBSERVATIONS:<br /><br />--p. 33: "There was this about the morning rush hour: The freeways were jammed, but not necessarily with idiots....Despite the stand-up comics who had made California drivers the joke of the world, they were much better on freeways than any people she had seen elsewhere" Can't say I disagree with that assessment.<br /><br />--p. 69: "People who got on 'The Tonight Show' were important." As Ed McMahon used to say to Johnny Carson (may they both RIP), "YES, HAW HAW HAW!! You are correct, sir!"<br /><br />--p. 78: Halley's Comet and BROOKS' Comet!! 😮<br /><br />--p. 87: Mayor Bentley Allen = Tom Bradley?<br /><br />--p. 108: "He drank at stiff attention. It was one way of showing disapproval of officers who drank before lunch." Eh, get the stick out of your arse, Sergeant Breslov of the Red Army!<br /><br />--p. 149: "'You don't tease your Russian counterpart about his name. Resist the temptation.'" Pieter Jakov, too damn funny! (But shouldn't that be Piotr?) <br /><br />--p. 156: "Space is not cold." Really? I was taught differently as a kid.<br />"A man generates more heat inside each cubic inch of his body than the Sun does in each cubic inch of its surface."<br /><br />--p. 158: "Saki" [sic] Shouldn't that be "sake?"<br /><br />--p. 159: Lilith, a stripper with a "heart-shaped face;" compare to S.A. Bailey's stripper Khandi with the heart-shaped ass!<br /><br />--p. 165: SAC-trained killer, hooah!<br /><br />--p. 166: Gen. Thomas Bambridge of SAC modelled after Curtis E. LeMay?<br /><br />--p. 176: Wow, the pre-PC days when people still said "Oriental" instead of "Asian!" Is Johnny Kim modelled after Mike Woo?<br /><br />--p. 210: Ah, back in the day when "gay" still meant "happy."<br /><br />--p. 240: Air Force cops didn't carry submachine guns back then (and nor do they now), they carried M-16 rifles.<br /><br />--p. 283: WIKIPEDIA LOOKUP Hottentots<br /><br />--p. 340: Marty Robbins, like the singer?<br /><br />--p: 459: "Army submachine guns?" Ahem, that should be Army *rifles*.<br /><br />--p. 497: Haha, a "Monty Python's Flying Circus" reference!<br /><br />CENTRAL CASTING: James Franco as Brig. Gen. Johnny Baker, Will Smith as Lt.<br />Col. Jellison, Kirsten Dunst or Laura Prepon as Maureen Jellison<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2795479331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2795479331" />
    <a title="Austin Prince" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7005820-austin-prince"><img alt="Austin Prince" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2795479331?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Austin Prince" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Austin Prince" href="/user/show/7005820-austin-prince">Austin Prince</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2795479331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7702734820488014328">TLDR: the dialogue is great, the set-up was good, the apocalypse was fun, and the ending was hopeful, but this book's regressive social opinions do not stand the test of time.<br/><br/>The allure of the post-apocalyptic genre does not arise solely from morbid curiosity, conservatism, or Michael Bay explosions. The characters even mention it in the beginning - it is the desire for personal meaning in your life. A society-ender shatters alienation. You may safely pause from keeping up with the Facebook Jone</span>
  <span id="freeText7702734820488014328" style="display:none">TLDR: the dialogue is great, the set-up was good, the apocalypse was fun, and the ending was hopeful, but this book's regressive social opinions do not stand the test of time.<br /><br />The allure of the post-apocalyptic genre does not arise solely from morbid curiosity, conservatism, or Michael Bay explosions. The characters even mention it in the beginning - it is the desire for personal meaning in your life. A society-ender shatters alienation. You may safely pause from keeping up with the Facebook Joneses, from workplace kaizen, from commutes, and from all the other soul traps the lawyers, accountants, and tech paradigm shifters have constructed for us. If life is Sisyphean anyway, then a world ender enables us to roll only the boulders that truly matter. It is enforced mindfulness, without the subscription service offer or preaching that always accompanies these messages. <br /><br />After the end of the world, you can rebuild it in the way of your choosing. And the way the authors' chose to build, destroy, and rebuild this world were fascinating in some respects and hopelessly regressive in others. <br /><br />The dialogue in the beginning was fantastic, being engaging enough to transport you to the dinner parties and boardrooms muddied only by irony's growing eclipse. The disaster itself was spectacular: plausible yet terrifying. The side story of the astronauts is almost a microcosm for what unfolds down on Earth. The civilization they attempt to build is interesting as well. <br /><br />The two major problems I have with this story (-1 star for each) are: 1. the story does not benefit from 70's-era sociology and 2. the war scene seemed too advanced and well-armed for a society so recently destroyed. <br /><br />I want to enunciate point number 1 a little more. Racism and sexism are abound in this book. As in most media from this era, every character is physically described only in as much as they do not resemble Superman. The default option for every character is strong, big, male, and Caucasian. Only deviations from this ideal are mentioned and, in many cases, these deviations serve as their sole characterizations. <br /><br />The only black characters are either criminals, government employees (leeches in the subtext of this novel), cannibalistic slaves (seriously!), or one astronaut who only got chosen for the space mission because of affirmative action. All women are described based on their attractiveness and none are allowed any agency or moments of genius without it being approved of or qualified by male characters. Granted, some of them are rocks of stability when their male mate falters, but even then they serve only to reorient men away from despair. The one exception might be the Russian MD astronaut, but even she is relegated to a masculine other after initial comments are made about her physical appearance. Pretty sure it fails the Bechdel test, too. <br /><br />My hope was that these characterizations were set-ups. They would be further evidence of a society gone wrong, that this would all disappear once the comet reminded everyone what truly mattered. This is what kept me going through to the end. But no, none of that ever resolved. In fact, it got worse as the story ended. One might argue that if tribalism could exist in our comfortable modernity, it would only be amplified after society's end. That is a fair, if depressing, point and would've been interesting (as all ideas expressed in sci-fi should be). But the social subtexts here could not have been plot devices because they were neither resolved nor explored. They were simply products of the authors' attitudes. And there is nothing futuristic about "ample wom[e]n" or Ebonics-talking thieves who also happen to be Muslim who also happen to be Black Panthers who also happen to become cannibals who also happen to become slaves at the end. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7702734820488014328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Olivia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2445786-olivia"><img alt="Olivia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245681908p2/2445786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1422620749?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia" href="/user/show/2445786-olivia">Olivia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2445786-olivia?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1422620749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10494180915435898159">Lucifer's Hammer is a reluctant four stars, don't run out and buy the book just based on that rating. It's without any doubt a very engaging read and once things get going I thought it was a captivating, dark, gritty and realistic read.<br/>A list of things that make this a reluctant four stars:<br/>- Written in 1977, there's racial stereotypes and gender stereotypes that might rub some people the wrong way.<br/>- The first half of the book is exposition and we meet an endless cast of characters and maybe it w</span>
  <span id="freeText10494180915435898159" style="display:none">Lucifer's Hammer is a reluctant four stars, don't run out and buy the book just based on that rating. It's without any doubt a very engaging read and once things get going I thought it was a captivating, dark, gritty and realistic read.<br />A list of things that make this a reluctant four stars:<br />- Written in 1977, there's racial stereotypes and gender stereotypes that might rub some people the wrong way.<br />- The first half of the book is exposition and we meet an endless cast of characters and maybe it wasn't necessary to include that many people?<br />- The portrayal of career driven women, hahaha, okay, 1977, fair enough, but still.<br />- The portrayal of black people, again uhm, okay 1977, then again, come on.<br />- The portrayal of Russians, okay, this one is fully allowed, the Cold War was still going in 1977.<br />- The comet doesn't actually collide with our planet until far far far into the book.<br /><br />But:<br />- Humanity goes and becomes nothing but cow-dung once the comet hits. We're talking low. Murder is just the beginning. I don't want to spoil just how low it all goes, but I thought it was interesting. I watch the news these days and look at the current refugee crisis and you know what? I fully believe humanity would sink this low if society fell apart and we neither had food, electricity nor anything else you need to survive an apocalypse. <br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed just how far Niven took it and maybe there's too many stereotypes and maybe he's too much of a white male in the way he approached this book, but that didn't stop me from reading this in almost one sitting.<br /><br />If you like exposition and a big cast of characters and a lot of buildup and would like to read about a comet hitting our planet? This one is for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10494180915435898159" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_889284'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/889284.The_Postman"><img alt="The Postman" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348914032l/889284._SY160_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oOP6A6Wg2VLar1BI0zTrsEeUnjuceWsPV2n1SJm3r0OsZcNmgypkAG7S2ZvmjfUDuU6bxUAl/7WE9KLY1qqPXQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12534.Larry_Niven">About Larry Niven</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12534.Larry_Niven"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1596428206p3/12534.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12534.Larry_Niven">Larry Niven
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,612
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4446621596134380637">Laurence van Cott Niven's best known work is <i><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61179.Ringworld' title='Ringworld' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ringworld</a> </i>(Ringworld, #1) (1970), which received the Hugo, Locus, Ditmar, and Nebula awards. His work is primarily hard science fiction, using big science concepts and theoretical physics. The creation of thoroughly worked-out alien species, which are very different from humans both physically and mentally, is recognized as one of Niven's main strengths</span>
  <span id="freeText4446621596134380637" style="display:none">Laurence van Cott Niven's best known work is <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61179.Ringworld" title="Ringworld" rel="nofollow noopener">Ringworld</a> </i>(Ringworld, #1) (1970), which received the Hugo, Locus, Ditmar, and Nebula awards. His work is primarily hard science fiction, using big science concepts and theoretical physics. The creation of thoroughly worked-out alien species, which are very different from humans both physically and mentally, is recognized as one of Niven's main strengths.<br /><br />Niven also often includes elements of detective fiction and adventure stories. His fantasy includes <i>The Magic Goes Away</i> series, which utilizes an exhaustible resource, called Mana, to make the magic a non-renewable resource. <br /><br />Niven created an alien species, the Kzin, which were featured in a series of twelve collection books, the Man-Kzin Wars. He co-authored a number of novels with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/39099.Jerry_Pournelle" title="Jerry Pournelle" rel="nofollow noopener">Jerry Pournelle</a>. In fact, much of his writing since the 1970s has been in collaboration, particularly with Pournelle, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/20058.Steven_Barnes" title="Steven Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">Steven Barnes</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/28045.Brenda_Cooper" title="Brenda Cooper" rel="nofollow noopener">Brenda Cooper</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/67927.Edward_M__Lerner" title="Edward M. Lerner" rel="nofollow noopener">Edward M. Lerner</a>.<br /><br />He briefly attended the California Institute of Technology and graduated with a Bachelor of Arts in mathematics (with a minor in psychology) from Washburn University, Topeka, Kansas, in 1962. He did a year of graduate work in mathematics at the University of California at Los Angeles. He has since lived in Los Angeles suburbs, including Chatsworth and Tarzana, as a full-time writer. He married Marilyn Joyce "Fuzzy Pink" Wisowaty, herself a well-known science fiction and Regency literature fan, on September 6, 1969.<br /><br />Niven won the Hugo Award for Best Short Story for <i>Neutron Star</i> in 1967. In 1972, for <i>Inconstant Moon</i>, and in 1975 for <i>The Hole Man</i>. In 1976, he won the Hugo Award for Best Novelette for <i>The Borderland of Sol</i>.<br /><br />Niven has written scripts for various science fiction television shows, including the original Land of the Lost series and Star Trek: The Animated Series, for which he adapted his early Kzin story <i>The Soft Weapon</i>. He adapted his story <i>Inconstant Moon</i> for an episode of the television series The Outer Limits in 1996.<br /><br />He has also written for the DC Comics character Green Lantern including in his stories hard science fiction concepts such as universal entropy and the redshift effect, which are unusual in comic books.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/larryniven">http://us.macmillan.com/author/larryn...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4446621596134380637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
